./server/blog-posts-data.json:1044:    "content": "Counselors are the caretakers of emotional well-being. They hold space for grief, guide people through trauma, and help others find clarity during confusion.\r\n\r\nBut who looks after the counselor?\r\n\r\nBurnout, compassion fatigue, and emotional exhaustion are very real challenges in the profession. You must understand that taking care of your clients starts with taking care of yourself.\r\n\r\nThis guide is for counselors who want to stay healthy, mentally, physically, and emotionally, so they can continue to show up with clarity and compassion.\r\n## 1. Recognizing the Signs of Burnout Before It Hits\n\r\nBurnout doesn\u2019t happen overnight. It builds slowly and quietly, often disguised as \u201cjust being tired\u201d or \u201chaving a rough week.\u201d Counselors may push through the warning signs, thinking they\u2019re simply part of the job. But ignoring persistent fatigue, irritability, lack of motivation, or emotional numbness can lead to deeper exhaustion.\r\n\r\nRecognizing these signs early can prevent long-term consequences. If you start to feel emotionally drained after sessions or dread going to work, that\u2019s a signal. It\u2019s not weakness, it\u2019s feedback from your body and mind, telling you it\u2019s time to pause and recharge.\r\n## 2. Advancing Your Education Without Burning Out\n\r\nMany counselors feel the need to pursue further education to grow their skills or meet licensing requirements. The idea of returning to school, especially with a full-time job or family, can be overwhelming. The good news is that you no longer need to step into a classroom to earn an advanced degree. Online counseling programs now offer flexibility without sacrificing quality.\r\n\r\nThe[ fastest online counseling degree](https://www.counselingdegreesonline.org/programs/accelerated-masters) is one that can be completed in less than two years. These fast-paced degrees allow you to balance work, study, and life without running yourself into the ground. You can log in during your lunch break or after hours, pacing your learning to fit your rhythm. Online learning is a smart, efficient way to keep growing without burning out.\r\n## 3. Building a Daily Routine That Works for You\n\r\nA steady routine can bring clarity and calm to your day. For counselors who often juggle back-to-back sessions, paperwork, and personal responsibilities, a thoughtful structure can make life feel more manageable. A good routine doesn\u2019t have to be complex; it just has to work for you.\r\n\r\nSleep well. Set aside short breaks between sessions, even if it\u2019s just five minutes to stretch or breathe. Schedule meals instead of skipping them or eating at your desk. Keep a buffer between work and personal time. These small habits can add up to a meaningful difference in your mood and focus.\r\n## 4. Therapy Isn\u2019t Just for Clients\n\r\nIt\u2019s easy to recommend therapy to others. But it\u2019s just as important for counselors to seek support when they need it. Carrying other people\u2019s pain day after day can become emotionally heavy. You might not even notice how much you're holding until it starts to affect your sleep, relationships, or work.\r\n\r\nHaving your own therapist gives you a space to process, reflect, and reset. If you\u2019ve been avoiding [therapy](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/treatments/therapy-treatment/) out of pride or fear, remember that even the best healers need healing. And showing up for yourself models the very thing you encourage in your clients: self-compassion.\r\n## 5. Movement, Not Just Mindfulness\n\r\nMindfulness is often the go-to self-care tool for mental health professionals, but it\u2019s not the only one. Movement plays a vital role in[ managing stress](https://www.cdc.gov/mental-health/living-with/index.html). It doesn\u2019t have to be intense or time-consuming. A walk around the block, a quick stretch between sessions, or dancing to music at home can do wonders for your mood and focus.\r\n\r\nPhysical movement helps release tension that talking alone can\u2019t touch. It activates your body in ways that reset your nervous system. Regular activity, even in short bursts, boosts energy, improves sleep, and increases resilience. Mindfulness is powerful, but when paired with movement, it becomes even more effective.\r\n## 6. Learning to Say No Without Guilt\n\r\nCounselors often fall into the habit of saying yes to everything, extra sessions, after-hours calls, weekend workshops. It comes from a good place: the desire to help. But overcommitting can quietly erode your energy and emotional availability.\r\n\r\nSetting boundaries can feel uncomfortable, especially when you fear disappointing someone. But burnout doesn\u2019t just harm you, it affects your ability to serve your clients well. A firm but kind \u201cno\u201d creates room to rest and recharge. You\u2019re not being selfish. You\u2019re being responsible with your energy.\r\n\r\nGood Read: [How to stop people pleasing](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/how-to-stop-people-pleasing/).\r\n## 7. Creating a Support Network of Fellow Professionals\n\r\nIt\u2019s hard to explain the emotional toll of counseling to someone outside the field. That\u2019s why having a community of fellow professionals matters. Peers can offer understanding that even close friends or family might not. Whether it\u2019s through a formal supervision group, peer consults, or casual conversations, connection helps.\r\n\r\nThese relationships offer more than just advice. They remind you that you\u2019re not alone in the struggles or the joys of the work. Venting about a tough session, brainstorming difficult cases, or even laughing together can provide powerful relief. Don\u2019t wait until you\u2019re burned out to reach out. Build those connections before you need them.\r\n\r\nGood Read: [Future of Healthcare](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/future-of-healthcare/)\r\n## 8. Reclaiming Joy and Creativity Outside of Work\n\r\nWhen your job centers on emotions and deep conversations, your downtime should feel light, fun, and personal. Counseling is meaningful work, but it shouldn\u2019t be your entire identity. Reconnecting with hobbies and interests that bring you joy can act as a reset button for your mind.\r\n\r\nMaybe you love to cook, sketch, garden, hike, or write. It doesn\u2019t need to be productive or impressive, it just needs to bring you pleasure.[ Creativity helps](https://www.wikihow.com/Be-Creative) you rediscover a part of yourself that doesn\u2019t have to carry anyone else.\r\n## 9. Setting Tech Boundaries in a Digital World\n\r\n[Virtual therapy](/virtual-counseling-services) has made access easier, but it\u2019s also blurred the lines between work and home. Constant emails, texts, and screen time can lead to digital fatigue. Without boundaries, you may find yourself checking messages late at night or feeling tethered to your phone.\r\n\r\nTherefore, set clear work hours and stick to them. Consider using separate devices or accounts for work and personal life. Digital boundaries are just as important as emotional ones.\r\n## 10. Eventually, the body and mind will force rest through illness, apathy, or burnout.\r\n\r\nStart small. Take five minutes to close your eyes. Schedule a weekend with no plans. It\u2019s the bedrock of ethical, sustainable practice. Healing means honoring your limits, prioritizing connection, creating balance, and giving yourself grace. It's the opposite of virtual or online interactions.\r\n\r\nWhen we talk about in person therapy, we mean meeting your therapist face-to-face in their office, rather than talking through a computer screen or phone.\r\n\r\nThe world changed a lot during the pandemic. Many people tried [online therapy](/virtual-counseling-services) for the first time. Then suddenly, virtual meetings became the norm, and many people shifted to remote work, online learning, and telehealth visits.\r\n\r\nWhile virtual sessions helped millions of people, many are now asking: \"Should I go back to in person therapy?\"\r\n\r\nThe answer isn't the same for everyone, but there are some clear benefits to meeting your therapist in person.\r\n## What Is the Meaning of In Person?\n\r\nIn person means showing up physically, not through a screen, text, or phone call. It\u2019s when you make the effort to go somewhere and be present face-to-face with someone. Going to a clinic in person is different from joining a virtual appointment. Meeting a friend at a caf\u00e9 feels more real than chatting over video.\r\n\r\nThe in person meaning goes beyond just location, it\u2019s about connection. Being physically present allows for deeper conversations, clearer communication, and a stronger human bond. In an in-person meeting, for example, people tend to feel more engaged, focused, and emotionally connected.\r\n\r\nIn therapy, that effort to show up in person can lead to more meaningful sessions, build trust more quickly, and provide real emotional support when it's needed most. If you're looking for in person synonyms, terms like **\"face-to-face,\"** **\"physically present,\"** or** \"on-site\"** are often used.\r\n## Why In Person Therapy Can Work Better\n\r\n[According to WebMD](https://www.webmd.com/mental-health/online-mental-health-therapy-what-to-know), \"Some people benefit more from in-person sessions. In online therapy, you lose aspects of nonverbal communication, like body language. These unspoken signals, like a tapping foot or fidgety hand, can help a therapist understand your overall mood.\"\r\n\r\nWhen you meet with your therapist in person, they can see your whole body, not just your face on a screen. They notice when you cross your arms, tap your foot, or slouch in your chair. These small actions tell your therapist a lot about how you're really feeling, even when your words say something different.\r\n\r\n[Psychology Today notes](https://www.psychologytoday.com/us/basics/therapy/what-works-best-online-or-in-person-therapy) that \"Therapists seem to think in-person therapy is slightly better than teletherapy, but if the trade-off is therapy vs. no therapy, then teletherapy is a much better course of action.\" This shows that while both types of therapy work, many professionals still see special value in meeting in person.\r\n### Sarah's Story: When In Person Made the Difference\n\r\nSarah had been doing online therapy for six months. She liked her therapist and felt comfortable talking on video calls. But something was missing. \"I felt like I was holding back,\" Sarah says. \"It was too easy to just close the laptop when things got hard.\"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen Sarah switched to in person sessions, everything changed. \"Being in that office, with my therapist right there, made me feel safer to talk about the really difficult stuff. I couldn't just click 'leave meeting' when I got uncomfortable. My therapist could see when I was getting upset and helped me work through it right away.\"\r\n\r\nSarah's experience shows how in person therapy can create a stronger sense of commitment and connection. When you have to travel to your appointment and sit in the same room as your therapist, it feels more real and important.\r\n## The Science Behind In Person Connections\n\r\nResearch shows that people connect better when they're in the same physical space. When you're in person with someone, your brain releases hormones that help you feel closer and more trusting. This is harder to achieve when you're looking at a screen.\r\n\r\nIn person therapy also helps your nervous system feel calm. Your office visits become a routine that your brain recognizes as a safe, healing time. Walking into your therapist's office signals to your mind: \"This is where I work on myself.\"\r\n## What You Get from In Person Therapy That You Can't Get Online\n\r\nBetter Focus: When you're in your therapist's office, there are no distractions. No pets jumping on your lap, no family members walking by, no work emails popping up on your screen. You can give your full attention to your mental health.\r\n\r\nStronger Safety: Some people worry about privacy when doing therapy from hom\r\n\r\ne. What if someone overhears? What if the video call isn't secure? In person therapy happens in a private, professional space designed just for healing.\r\n\r\nFull Communication: Your therapist can see everything - how you sit, how you breathe, how you move your hands when you talk. This helps them understand you better and give you more helpful support.\r\n\r\nReal Rituals: The act of traveling to therapy, sitting in the waiting room, and walking into your therapist's office creates a mental shift. It tells your brain: \"This is an important time for me.\"\r\n### **In Person vs Online Therapy Comparison**\n\r\nWhen choosing between in person and online therapy, it helps to see them side by side.\r\n\r\nHere's how they compare across the most important factors:\r\n\r\n\r\n### Additional Research Shows In Person Session Works\n\r\nThe numbers don\u2019t lie.\r\n\r\nTherapy works, and many people are turning to it for support. Since 2020, about 30% of American adults have seen a therapist, and in 2021 alone, around 42 million people received mental health treatment or counseling.\r\n\r\nWhen it comes to outcomes, the research is encouraging. One major study found that 67.98% of people improved with therapy, 33.83% showed significant improvement, and 34.15% showed some progress. The dropout rate was just 18.76%, meaning most people stick with therapy once they find the right fit.\r\n\r\n[Cognitive Behavioral Therapy](/cognitive-behavioral-therapy) (CBT), one of the most common approaches used in both in-person and [virtual therapy](/virtual-counseling-services), shows success rates between 50% and 75% for issues like anxiety, depression, and phobias.\r\n\r\n\u00a0\r\n\r\nLearn About In Person Therapy Here\r\n## Mental Conditions That Benefit Most from In Person Sessions\n\r\nCertain mental health conditions respond better to in person treatment.\r\n\r\nHere are the main ones where meeting face-to-face often makes a bigger difference:\r\n\r\nPTSD and Trauma Disorders: When you've experienced trauma, your nervous system is often on high alert. Being in a safe, consistent physical space with your therapist helps your brain learn that you're truly safe. In person therapy allows therapists to use body-based techniques that help process trauma stored in your body.\r\n\r\nSevere Depression: When depression is severe, it can be hard to feel connected to anything or anyone. The physical presence of your therapist during [depression therapy](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/treatments/depression-therapy/) can provide a lifeline when you feel completely alone. It's also easier for therapists to assess suicide risk when they can see you in person.\r\n\r\n[Eating Disorders](/services): Recovery from [eating disorders](/services) often involves changing your relationship with your body. [In person therapy](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/therapy/in-person-therapy-orlando/) lets therapists observe eating behaviors, body language, and physical signs that might indicate relapse or progress.\r\n\r\nSevere Anxiety and Panic Disorders: Panic attacks can feel terrifying, especially when you're alone at home. Having your therapist physically present during anxiety episodes can provide immediate grounding and safety. They can guide you through breathing exercises and calming techniques more effectively.\r\n\r\nAutism Spectrum Disorders: Many people with autism benefit from the structure and routine of in person appointments. The consistent environment helps reduce anxiety, and therapists can better observe and work with sensory needs.\r\n\r\nPersonality Disorders: Building trust and healthy relationships is often a key part of treating personality disorders. The deeper connection possible through in person therapy can be crucial for healing relationship patterns.\r\n\r\nSubstance Use Disorders: Accountability is important in addiction recovery. In person sessions make it harder to avoid appointments and easier for therapists to notice signs of relapse.\r\n## \n\r\n### **What Therapists Say About In Person Sessions**\n\r\nMental health professionals have strong opinions about the value of face-to-face therapy. Dr. Robert Glenn, a licensed clinical psychologist in Orlando, explains: \"There's an energy exchange that happens when you're physically present with someone that you just can't replicate through a screen. I can sense my client's nervous system state much more accurately when they're sitting across from me.\"\r\n\r\nLicensed therapist Karla Mcleod notes: \"In my 8 years of practice, I've seen how the ritual of coming to therapy like the drive, the waiting room, the transition into my office, actually helps people shift into therapeutic mode. It's harder to achieve that mental shift when you're logging in from your kitchen table.\"\r\n### The Home Environment Challenge\n\r\nYour home is full of distractions, even when you don't realize it. During online therapy, part of your brain might be thinking about the dishes in the sink, the laundry that needs folding, or the work deadline you're stressed about.\r\n\r\nWhen you go to your therapist's office for in person sessions, you leave all those distractions behind. The only thing you need to focus on is your mental health. This can make your therapy time much more effective.\r\n### Building Trust Through In Person Connection\n\r\nTrust is the most important part of therapy. You need to feel safe with your therapist before you can share your deepest thoughts and feelings. While you can build trust online, it often happens faster in person.\r\n\r\nWhen you're in the same room as your therapist, you can see their full reactions. You notice their body language, their facial expressions, and their energy. This helps you feel more confident that they truly care about helping you.\r\n\r\nIn person meetings also feel more \"real\" to many people. It's easier to believe that your therapist is fully present and focused on you when they're sitting across from you, rather than appearing as a face on a screen that could be distracted by other things.\r\n## In Person: Making the Right Choice for You\n\r\nThe best therapy is the therapy you actually do. If online sessions work well for you and you're making progress, that's great! But if you've been struggling with [virtual therapy](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/treatments/virtual-counseling/) or feeling like something is missing, it might be time to try in person sessions.\r\n\r\nThink about these questions:\r\n\r\n \tDo you find it hard to focus during online sessions?\r\n \tDo you feel like you're holding back or not fully opening up?\r\n \tAre you dealing with trauma or crisis situations?\r\n \tDo you miss the human connection of being in the same room as someone?\r\n \tAre distractions at home making it hard to get the most out of therapy?\r\n\r\n### When In Person Therapy Is Especially Helpful\n\r\nBeyond specific mental health conditions, some situations make in person therapy particularly valuable:\r\n\r\n \tWhen you're in crisis: If you're having thoughts of hurting yourself or others, being physically present with a trained professional provides an extra layer of safety.\r\n \tIf you have trouble focusing at home: Many people find it hard to concentrate during online sessions because of distractions in their environment.\r\n \tIf you're not comfortable with technology: Some people feel awkward on video calls or worry about technical problems interrupting important conversations.\r\n\r\nIf you answered yes to any of these, in person therapy might be a better fit for you.\r\n## Showing Up In Person: The Bottom Line\n\r\nIn person therapy offers something special that's hard to replicate online. They allow you to move at your own pace, create a more manageable routine, and work in an environment where you feel safe and in control.\r\n\r\nRelated Story: [Top 10 Best Low-Stress Jobs: Great Pay, Happy Mental Health](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/top-10-best-low-stress-jobs/)\r\n## **How Do People With Depression Hold a Job?**\n\r\nYou might be wondering, \u201cIs it even possible to work when I feel this low?\u201d\r\n\r\n\r\nThe honest answer is: yes, it is possible\u2014but it can look different for everyone.\r\n\r\nSome days might feel heavier than others. Allow yourself to seek a job that fits your needs, not just financially, but emotionally.\r\n\r\n\r\n### Why Remote Jobs Work Well for People With Depression\n\r\nFor many people living with depression, traditional 9-to-5 office jobs can feel especially challenging. That\u2019s where remote work can offer real relief, and studies support these claims.\r\n\r\nAccording to a study published in the [Journal of Occupational and Environmental Medicine](https://journals.lww.com/joem/pages/default.aspx), remote work can provide a better work-life balance and reduce stress, which may be healthy for individuals managing depression.\r\n\r\nAnother 2022 study by Owl Labs and Global Workplace Analytics found that 62% of employees feel they can get more done when working from home. Also, 52% said they would accept a small pay cut to have the option to work remotely. This shows that many people really value the better work-life balance that remote work offers. ([Forbes](https://www.forbes.com/councils/forbeshumanresourcescouncil/2023/07/03/how-remote-work-can-impact-employees-mental-health/#:~:text=According%20to%20a,work%2Dlife%20balance))\r\n\r\nRemote jobs provide two key benefits: flexibility and independence. These aren\u2019t just nice-to-haves\u2014they can be essential when you're managing your mental health.\r\n#### Flexibility\n\r\nOne of the most valuable aspects of remote work is the ability to adjust your schedule. Many remote roles offer flexible hours, which can be a game-changer if you struggle with energy dips, difficulty sleeping, or low motivation, especially in the mornings. Being able to start your day a bit later, take short breaks when you need them, or fit in a therapy appointment without rearranging your whole day can make work more manageable and less stressful.\r\n\r\nThis kind of flexibility gives you room to prioritise your wellbeing, without sacrificing your productivity.\r\n#### Independence\n\r\nRemote work often allows for more autonomy, meaning you have greater control over your daily tasks and work environment. While you\u2019ll still stay connected with colleagues virtually, the reduced face-to-face interaction can help ease social anxiety and limit the emotional drain that often comes from navigating a busy office setting.\r\n\r\nYou can set up your workspace to feel calming and supportive\u2014whether that means working in silence, playing music that lifts your mood, or simply being in a space that feels safe and familiar.\r\n\u201cRemote jobs aren't a cure for depression, but they can create a work environment that feels more manageable, less triggering, and better aligned with your mental health needs.\u201d - **Keith Harryhill of Empathy Health Clinic**\r\n[\r\nConfused If You're Depressed? On these days, it's important to be compassionate with yourself and prioritize your well-being. Pushing through severe depression without acknowledging how you feel can make things worse in the long run.\r\n\r\nHere are some strategies that might help when you're trying to work while feeling severely depressed:\r\n### Communicate (If You Feel Safe and Able)\n\r\nIf you have a supportive employer and feel safe doing so, consider letting them know you're having a difficult time. You don't need to share every detail, but a simple \"I'm having a challenging day with my health and may need some flexibility\" can open the door for understanding and potential adjustments.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIf you have an HR department, they might be a good resource to talk about options like taking a leave of absence if needed.\r\n\r\nThis is also a good time to reflect on [how to stop people pleasing](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/how-to-stop-people-pleasing/), especially if you find yourself constantly saying \u201cyes\u201d to avoid disappointing others, even when you're overwhelmed. Prioritising your mental health sometimes means setting boundaries and asking for support without guilt.\r\n\r\nLearning to advocate for your needs is a powerful step toward both healing and maintaining your well-being at work.\r\n### Focus on Small, Essential Tasks\n\r\nWhen everything feels overwhelming, break your work down into the absolute smallest, most necessary tasks. What is the one thing that absolutely has to get done today?\r\n\r\nFocus only on that. **Completing even one small task** can feel like a huge accomplishment when you're severely depressed.\r\n### Lower Your Expectations\n\r\nThis is not the time to aim for peak productivity or creativity. **Lower your expectations for yourself**. It's okay if you're not working at your usual pace or producing your best work. The goal is simply to get through the essential parts of the workday as gently as possible.\r\n### Take Frequent Breaks\n\r\nDon't try to power through for hours on end. Take short, frequent breaks. **Step away from your workspace**, do some stretching, listen to calming music, or just close your eyes for a few minutes. These breaks can help prevent burnout and make the work periods more manageable.\r\n### Create a Supportive Work Environment (Even at Home)\n\r\nSince you're working remotely, try to make your workspace as comfortable and supportive as possible. This might mean adjusting the lighting, having comforting items nearby, or playing soft, instrumental music. **Minimize distractions** as much as possible.\r\n### Consider Taking Time Off\n\r\nIf your severe depression is making it nearly impossible to function, it might be necessary to take a sick day or a short-term leave of absence. Your mental health is just as important as your physical health, and sometimes you need time to focus on getting better without the added pressure of work.\r\n\r\nLook into your **company's policies** regarding sick leave or disability.\r\n### Revisit Your Treatment Plan\n\r\n**Severe depression** is a sign that you may need to check in with your mental health professional. It might be time to adjust your treatment plan, try a different approach, or increase the frequency of your therapy sessions.\r\n\r\nWorking when severely depressed is incredibly challenging. **Be kind to yourself,** prioritize your well-being, and utilize your support systems.\r\n## **Signs a Job Isn\u2019t Good for Your Mental Health**\n\r\nSome jobs can make depression worse. Did you know that about 5.6% of adults in the U.S. identify as LGBTQ+? (Source:[ NBC News Poll](https://www.nbcnews.com/feature/nbc-out/americans-are-identifying-lgbtq-more-ever-poll-finds-n1258627)) That\u2019s millions of people!\n\nIn this blog, we\u2019ll explore what LGBTQIA2S+ means, its core identities, and key concepts. Understanding these identities is crucial for fostering mental well-being and combating stigma. By learning together, we can create a more inclusive and compassionate society.\u00a0\n\nWe'll also talk about the significance of LGBTQ+ affirmative therapy and how it aligns with our mission to provide a safe, supportive space for everyone. By addressing the unique needs of LGBTQ+ individuals, we aim to promote mental health and well-being for all.\n\nAre you ready to learn something new and celebrate diversity? Let\u2019s get started!\n\n\u00a0\n\n## What does LGBTQIA2S+ stand for?\u00a0\nLGBTQIA2S+ is more than just an acronym\u2014it\u2019s a celebration of diverse identities. Each letter represents a unique group with different experiences and stories. Curious about what they all mean?\n\nLet\u2019s break it down together:\u00a0\n\nL: Lesbian\u00a0G: Gay\u00a0B: Bisexual\u00a0T: Transgender\u00a0Q: Queer or Questioning\u00a0I: Intersex\u00a0A: Asexual or Ally\u00a02S: Two-Spirit (a term from Indigenous cultures)\u00a0\u00a0\u2026with the \u201c+\u201d representing additional identities that are not specifically mentioned.\n\nBy understanding these terms, we take an important step toward creating a world where everyone is respected for who they are. Join us in learning, unlearning, and embracing the beauty of individuality!\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Lesbian\u00a0\nA lesbian is a woman who loves and is attracted to other women. This identity is a strong and beautiful part of the LGBTQIA2S+ community. Some people mistakenly think lesbians can\u2019t have close friendships with men, but that\u2019s just not true. Being a lesbian is about who you love, not who you can be friends with.\u00a0\n\nSeeing lesbians represented in the world is so important. It helps break stereotypes and shows that same-sex love is natural and powerful. Whenever the media shares real and diverse stories about lesbians, it helps everyone understand and celebrate their lives.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Gay\nThe term **gay** usually refers to men who love and are attracted to other men. Their voices have played a huge role in fighting for LGBTQIA2S+ rights, from the [Stonewall riots](https://www.history.com/topics/lgbtq/the-stonewall-riots) to today\u2019s pride celebrations. These movements show strength, courage, and love.\u00a0\n\nWhile society has come a long way in accepting gay people, there\u2019s still work to do. Seeing gay people represented in movies, shows, and culture helps break down old biases and shows the beauty of love in all its forms.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Bisexual\u00a0\nBisexual people are attracted to two or more genders. This identity is open and dynamic, showing that love and attraction can take many forms. Sadly, some people wrongly think being bisexual means being \u201cconfused,\u201d but that\u2019s simply not true. Bisexuality is real, valid, and deserves respect.\u00a0\n\nSometimes bisexual people face \"[biphobia](https://www.medicalnewstoday.com/articles/biphobia),\" even within LGBTQIA2S+ spaces, which can make them feel invisible. By recognizing and celebrating bisexuality, we can make sure their voices are heard and valued.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Transgender\u00a0\nTransgender people are those whose gender is different from the one they were assigned at birth. Gender identity is about who you are inside, and it\u2019s separate from who you\u2019re attracted to. Transgender people can be heterosexual, gay, bisexual, or have another orientation\u2014it\u2019s all valid.\u00a0\n\nTransitioning can mean things like changing your name, how you dress, or using medical or legal steps. It\u2019s a personal journey, and everyone\u2019s path is different. Understanding and supporting transgender people means respecting their identity and challenging the unfair rules society sometimes tries to impose.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Queer/Questioning\u00a0\nThe word \u201cqueer\u201d is a powerful term for people who don\u2019t fit into traditional ideas about gender or sexuality. It\u2019s been reclaimed by many as a positive and proud identity, even though it was once used as an insult. Some people may still prefer not to use it, and that\u2019s okay too.\u00a0\n\nFor those questioning who they are, the \u201cQ\u201d in LGBTQIA2S+ is a safe space. Questioning isn\u2019t about being unsure\u2014it\u2019s about exploring and discovering who you truly are. It\u2019s a journey of courage, self-love, and growth, and it\u2019s a beautiful part of finding your place in the world.\n\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t## Beyond the Basics: Exploring Additional Identities\u00a0\n### Intersex\n[Intersex](https://my.clevelandclinic.org/health/articles/16324-intersex) refers to individuals born with natural variations in sex characteristics that don\u2019t fit typical definitions of male or female. These traits occur in about 1.7% of the population, highlighting the diversity of human biology.\n\nHistorically, intersex individuals have faced marginalization and non-consensual medical interventions aimed at conforming to binary norms, causing lasting harm. The intersex rights movement now advocates for bodily autonomy, informed consent, and ending unnecessary medical procedures, particularly for children.\n\nKey issues include legal recognition beyond binary gender categories, access to unbiased healthcare, and promoting visibility. Recognizing and respecting intersex people challenges societal norms and fosters greater inclusion.\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Asexual\u00a0\nAsexual individuals experience little to no sexual attraction to others, though they may still form romantic or emotional connections. It\u2019s important to distinguish asexuality from celibacy, which is a voluntary choice, while asexuality is an orientation.\u00a0\n\nAsexuality exists on a spectrum, encompassing identities like demisexuality (experiencing attraction only after a strong emotional connection) and graysexuality (experiencing attraction infrequently). Allowing autonomy over one\u2019s sexuality fosters inclusivity.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Two-Spirit\u00a0\nSo, what is the meaning of two-spirited? It's a term used by Indigenous communities to describe individuals embodying both masculine and feminine spirits. This identity holds significant cultural and spiritual importance and must be understood within the context of Indigenous traditions.\u00a0\n\nRespecting Two-Spirit identities involves acknowledging the colonial harm that affected Indigenous communities and supporting their efforts to revive and preserve cultural wisdom.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### The + in LGBTQIA2S+\u00a0\nThe \"+\" symbolizes inclusivity. It ensures all identities falling outside the listed terms have a place under this umbrella, such as pansexuality, non-binary identities, and more.\u00a0\n\nLanguage may not capture the entirety of human diversity, but the \"+\" reminds us there\u2019s always room to grow in our understanding and inclusion.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n## Understanding Gender, Sexuality, and Identity: Key Ideas\u00a0\nWhether it's LGBT, LGBTQ, or LGBTQIA2S+, the community continues to grow and evolve. Let's dig deeper into the key concepts that define and unite this vibrant and diverse group.\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Gender Identity and Sexual Orientation\u00a0\nGender identity is about a person\u2019s inner sense of their own gender. This could be man, woman, non-binary, or genderqueer. It may or may not match the sex they were assigned at birth.\u00a0\n\nSexual orientation, on the other hand, is about who someone is attracted to emotionally, romantically, or sexually. Examples include heterosexual (straight), gay, bisexual, or asexual.\u00a0\n\nThese two concepts are different but often overlap. For example, non-binary people may face unique challenges tied to both their gender identity and who they\u2019re attracted to.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Going Beyond Cisgender and Non-Binary\u00a0\nCisgender means a person\u2019s gender matches the sex they were assigned at birth. But gender is not limited to just \"male\" or \"female.\"\u00a0\n\nNon-binary is a broad category and includes many identities. Some examples are:\u00a0\n\nAgender: Feeling no connection to any gender.\u00a0Bigender: Identifying as two genders.\u00a0Genderfluid: Having a gender identity that changes over time.\u00a0\u00a0Some Indigenous cultures have their own unique ways of seeing gender. For example, the Two-Spirit identity blends gender and spirituality, offering a non-Western view of gender diversity.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### The Importance of Pronouns\u00a0\nPronouns like he/him, she/her, and they/them help affirm a person\u2019s gender. Some people also use neopronouns like xe/xem or fae/faer to better reflect their identity.\u00a0\n\nUsing correct pronouns shows respect. Communities and organizations can normalize this by sharing pronouns during introductions or adding them in email signatures.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Chosen Families\u00a0\nFor many LGBTQIA2S+ people, chosen families are their main source of love and support. This is especially true when biological families are not accepting.\u00a0\n\nChosen families can be even more important for people from marginalized groups, like BIPOC LGBTQIA2S+ individuals, who may face extra discrimination. These families often provide emotional and physical support, especially as people age.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Challenges and Issues Today\u00a0\nThere are still many challenges for LGBTQIA2S+ individuals. Some examples include:\u00a0\n\nDiscrimination in healthcare, such as a lack of access to gender-affirming care.\u00a0Anti-LGBTQIA2S+ laws in some places.\u00a0The erasure of diverse identities in media and conversations.\u00a0\u00a0Globally, some countries still criminalize LGBTQIA2S+ identities, while others are making progress toward equality.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Moving Forward\u00a0\nBy looking deeper into these topics, we can better understand the complexities of gender, sexuality, and identity. Recognizing these diverse experiences helps us create a more inclusive and fair world for everyone.\n\n\u00a0\n\n## The Social Stigma Around the LGBTQIA2S+ Community\u00a0\nThe LGBTQIA2S+ community still faces challenges because of social stigma. This affects many parts of their lives. Though there has been progress with more rights and acceptance, discrimination and unfair treatment are still common.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Challenges of Coming Out\u00a0\nMany people don\u2019t fully understand or accept LGBTQIA2S+ individuals. When someone comes out, they might face judgment or rejection. Sometimes, even family, friends, or coworkers are unkind.\u00a0\n\nThis can lead to feelings of loneliness, sadness, or anxiety. It can make it hard for them to live as their true selves.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Struggles in Relationships\u00a0\nStigma also creates problems in relationships. LGBTQIA2S+ couples often face judgement and harassment when showing love in public. Some families may not accept their relationships. This lack of support can be very hurtful.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\nGood Read: [How To Know if You Are Dating Someone with BPD](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/dating-someone-with-bpd/)\n\nWithout family support, couples often feel they have to manage things on their own. This is hard. Especially when you compare how straight couples get more help, love, and support from their family and friends in contrast to binary couples.\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Dating Difficulties\u00a0\nDating can also be challenging for this spectrum, and that's all because of fear of judgment and rejection. In less accepting societies, it might feel impossible to find a partner.\u00a0\n\nOn top of this, there are also those LGBTQIA2S+ individuals who struggle to accept themselves. This makes it even harder to build healthy relationships.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\n### Impact on Mental Health and Happiness\u00a0\nThe stigma doesn\u2019t just affect relationships. It takes a significant toll on mental health, job opportunities, and overall happiness. Constantly facing judgment and prejudice can lead to [social exhaustion](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/understanding-social-exhaustion-adhd-brain/), as individuals feel the need to overperform or mask their true selves to fit in.\n\nThis strain often leads to[ heightened anxiety](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/signs-of-crippling-anxiety/), feelings of isolation, and even [depression](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/what-is-functional-depression/), whether high-functioning or not. It can also hinder professional growth, as biases may restrict opportunities or contribute to workplace discrimination. While efforts to drive positive change are increasing, much work remains to address these deeply innate issues and build a genuinely inclusive society.\n\n\u00a0\n\n### A Better Future\u00a0\nFor a brighter future, we need to focus on kindness and understanding. Everyone deserves space to live and love freely.\u00a0\n\nOnly then can LGBTQIA2S+ individuals truly thrive in every part of their lives.\n\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t## Building Bridges: Promoting Understanding and Allyship\u00a0\nAllyship is about becoming an active supporter of LGBTQIA2S+ rights. Here\u2019s how you can foster inclusivity:\n\nUse inclusive language: Be mindful of your words and avoid assumptions about others\u2019 identities.\u00a0Respect privacy: Don\u2019t ask invasive questions about someone\u2019s gender, transition, or experience.\u00a0Educate yourself: Seek resources to deepen your understanding without placing the burden of education on LGBTQIA2S+ individuals.\u00a0### \u00a0\n### The Importance of LGBT Inclusive Therapy\nInclusive therapy is vital in creating safe, affirming spaces for LGBTQIA2S+ individuals to address mental health challenges. ./server/blog-posts-data.json:1988:    "content": "Introduction: Why We Need to Talk About This\n\n\u201cFor years, Sarah thought she was just disorganized. She was always late, always losing things. But when she forgot her best friend\u2019s wedding date\u2014something she\u2019d been excited about for months\u2014she knew something wasn\u2019t right. It wasn\u2019t about willpower or caring. It was something deeper: ADHD.\u201d\n\nMany people live their whole lives feeling like this without understanding why.\u00a0\n\nThey might label themselves as \u201clazy\u201d or \u201cscatterbrained,\u201d or they might even hear those words from others. But what\u2019s happening isn\u2019t about willpower or intelligence. The root cause is untreated ADHD (Attention-Deficit/Hyperactivity Disorder).\n\n[ADHD](https://www.psychiatry.org/patients-families/adhd/what-is-adhd) doesn\u2019t just make it hard to stay focused or organized. When left untreated, it can lead to feelings of worry, fear, and sadness that take over your life. That\u2019s because ADHD doesn\u2019t only affect how you think\u2014it affects how you feel.\u00a0\n\nAnxiety creeps in when you\u2019re afraid you\u2019ll mess up again. Depression follows when you feel like nothing will ever change.\n\nBut you\u2019re not broken.\u00a0\n\nADHD, anxiety, and depression are real struggles, but they\u2019re also treatable.\n\nThis article is here to help you understand what\u2019s going on inside you.\u00a0\n\nIt\u2019s not about giving you a \u201cquick fix\u201d or blaming you for what you\u2019re feeling. It\u2019s about shining a light on the connections between ADHD, anxiety, and depression, so you can stop feeling stuck and start feeling understood.\n\nAs one of my patients once told me: \u201cWhen I learned what was going on with me, it felt like I could breathe for the first time.\u201d\n\nSo today, we\u2019re going to explore:\n\nThe signs of ADHD in adults that often go unnoticed.\nHow ADHD impacts mental health, including its connection to anxiety and depression.\nPractical, evidence-based coping strategies for ADHD and anxiety.\n\n\u00a0\n\nIf you\u2019re ready to explore this journey, keep reading. By the end, we hope you\u2019ll feel less afraid to ask for help and more hopeful about the future. Because you deserve to feel better, and it\u2019s possible.\n\nLet\u2019s begin.\n\n## \u00a0\n\n## Chapter 1: What Is ADHD Really Like?\n\n\u201cImagine being stuck in a traffic jam, but the traffic is your thoughts. No matter how much you try, you can\u2019t move forward the way you want to.\u201d\n\nThat\u2019s what living with ADHD often feels like for adults. Most people think ADHD is something only kids deal with\u2014wiggly, noisy, and unable to sit still in class. But ADHD is more than just being \u201chyper\u201d or distracted. It\u2019s a neurological condition that affects how the brain works. Many adults live with undiagnosed ADHD, never realizing it\u2019s at the root of their struggles.\n\n### The Science Behind ADHD\n\nADHD, short for Attention-Deficit/Hyperactivity Disorder, is a condition that affects the brain\u2019s executive function system.\u00a0\n\nExecutive functions are like the brain\u2019s manager, helping you:\n\nPlan and organize tasks.\nStay focused on what matters.\nControl impulses.\nManage time effectively.\n\nIn people with ADHD, the brain has difficulty regulating certain chemicals, especially dopamine.\u00a0\n\nDopamine is a neurotransmitter that helps with motivation, focus, and the reward system. When [dopamine levels are imbalanced](https://www.medicalnewstoday.com/articles/320637), it can feel like your brain is constantly switching channels, making it hard to focus or follow through on tasks.\n\nAnother part of the brain affected by ADHD is the prefrontal cortex, which plays a big role in decision-making and self-control. In people with ADHD, this area doesn\u2019t activate the same way, leading to challenges like:\n\nForgetting important things (even when you try hard to remember).\nGetting easily distracted by noise, movement, or even your thoughts.\nFeeling stuck when faced with complex tasks.\n\n### \u00a0\n\n### ADHD Comes in Different Forms\n\nNot everyone with ADHD experiences it the same way. Researchers recognize three main types:\n\nInattentive Type: Difficulty focusing, forgetting details, and seeming \u201cspacey.\u201d\nHyperactive-Impulsive Type: Fidgeting, interrupting others, and struggling to sit still.\nCombined Type: A mix of inattentive and hyperactive symptoms.\n\n\u00a0\n\nUnderstanding these types can help you see how ADHD might affect you\u2014or someone you care about\u2014in unique ways. For many, [overcoming concentration and focus issues](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/concentration-and-focus/) is the start.\n\n### \u00a0\n\n### Signs of ADHD in Adults\n\nAdult ADHD can be tricky to spot because it often looks different than it does in children. Some common signs include:\n\nLosing your keys, phone, or important papers frequently.\nProcrastinating on tasks until you\u2019re overwhelmed by the pressure.\nStarting projects with enthusiasm but struggling to finish them.\nInterrupting conversations or speaking without thinking.\nAvoiding boring or repetitive tasks, even when they\u2019re important.\n\n\u00a0\n\nThese struggles often make people feel ashamed or frustrated.\u00a0\n\nOver time, this can lead to emotional issues like anxiety and depression, especially when they don\u2019t realize ADHD is the root cause.\n\n### \u00a0\n\n### How ADHD Impacts Mental Health\n\nWhen untreated, ADHD doesn\u2019t just cause missed deadlines or forgotten grocery lists. It can deeply affect your emotional health. Here\u2019s how:\n\nOverwhelm: Small tasks, like paying bills or replying to emails, can feel like mountains.\nLow Self-Esteem: Repeated failures or missed opportunities may make you feel like you\u2019re \u201cnot good enough.\u201d\nEmotional Sensitivity: Many people with ADHD experience intense emotions and have trouble regulating them.\n\n\u00a0\n\nOver time, these challenges can create a ripple effect, leading to symptoms of anxiety or depression.\n\n\u201cFor years, I felt like I was constantly disappointing myself and everyone around me. I didn\u2019t know why I couldn\u2019t just do the things other people seemed to do so easily.\u201d \u2014 A patient describing life with undiagnosed ADHD.\n\nBy understanding these signs, you\u2019re already taking an important step toward breaking the cycle.\u00a0\n\nIn the next chapter, we\u2019ll explore how [ADHD and anxiety often go hand in hand](https://www.healthline.com/health/adhd-and-anxiety), creating challenges that can feel overwhelming\u2014but aren\u2019t impossible to manage.\n\n## \u00a0\n\n## Chapter 2: The Anxiety That Comes With ADHD\n\n\u201cImagine this: You\u2019ve promised your boss a report, but you forgot the deadline. Now it\u2019s late, and you\u2019re scrambling to explain. Your heart races, your chest tightens, and all you can think is: \u2018Why can\u2019t I just be better at this?\u2019\u201d\n\nAnxiety feels like a constant companion for many people with ADHD. It\u2019s that nagging worry that you\u2019ll mess up again or that people will see through your struggles.\n\n### Does ADHD Cause Anxiety?\n\nADHD doesn\u2019t directly \u201ccause\u201d anxiety, but the two are deeply connected.\u00a0\n\nThink about it: ADHD can make everyday tasks harder, from remembering deadlines to maintaining relationships. When life feels unpredictable or out of control, it\u2019s natural to feel anxious.\n\nSome common reasons ADHD might lead to anxiety include:\n\nFear of Failure: \u201cWhat if I mess up again?\u201d\nSocial Anxiety: \u201cWill they think I\u2019m lazy or weird because I forgot something?\u201d\nFeeling Overwhelmed: Too many tasks, not enough time, and no clear starting point.\n\n### \u00a0\n\n### Anxiety Symptoms For People With ADHD\n\nThis constant state of worry takes a toll on your body and mind. People with ADHD often experience the physical symptoms of anxiety, like:\n\nRacing heart.\nSweaty palms.\nTightness in the chest.\nTrouble sleeping.\n\n### \u00a0\n\n### Coping Strategies for ADHD and Anxiety\n\nWhen ADHD and anxiety overlap, it can feel like you\u2019re stuck in a loop. ADHD makes it hard to stay on top of things, which causes anxiety. Then anxiety makes it harder to focus, which worsens ADHD symptoms.\n\nBut here\u2019s the good news: some strategies can help.\n\n-  Create a Routine:\nPredictability can reduce anxiety. Start small\u2014pick one or two daily habits to focus on, like setting reminders for appointments or planning your day the night before.\n\n-  Practice Mindfulness:\nMindfulness doesn\u2019t mean you have to meditate for hours. It can be as simple as taking five deep breaths when you feel overwhelmed. This helps your brain reset.\n\n-  Break Tasks Into Smaller Steps:\nBig tasks feel less scary when you break them down. Instead of \u201cclean the house,\u201d try \u201cpick up clothes in the bedroom for 10 minutes.\u201d\n\n-  Get Support:\nWhether it\u2019s an [Orlando Psychiatrist](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/), coach, or support group, connecting with others can make a huge difference.\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\nSupport groups connect you with others who understand your struggles, offering validation, shared experiences, and encouragement.\u00a0\n\nReaching out doesn\u2019t mean you\u2019re weak\u2014it\u2019s a step toward healing. Professionals and peers alike can help you feel supported, understood, and empowered to take control of your life.\u00a0\n\nLearn more here about [How to Focus Better with ADHD](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/how-to-focus-better-with-adhd/).\n\n### \u00a0\n\n### Best Therapy for ADHD and Anxiety\n\nThe most effective treatments often combine therapy and, when appropriate, medication.\n\n[Cognitive Behavioral Therapy](/cognitive-behavioral-therapy) (CBT): Helps you manage negative thoughts and build better habits. If you are interested in learning more about CBT, [click here](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/treatments/cognitive-behavioral-therapy/).\nMedication: This can improve focus and reduce symptoms of ADHD, making anxiety easier to manage.\n\n\u00a0\n\nRemember: Asking for help isn\u2019t a sign of weakness\u2014it\u2019s a step toward taking control of your life.\n\nIn the next chapter, we\u2019ll talk about how ADHD can lead to feelings of sadness and hopelessness, and what you can do to start feeling better.\n\n## \u00a0\n\n## Chapter 3: When ADHD Feels Like Depression\n\nLiving with untreated ADHD can feel like running a race where the finish line keeps moving.\u00a0\n\nThe constant struggle to meet expectations. Your own and others\u2019\u2014can take an emotional toll.\u00a0\n\nOver time, the frustration and overwhelm can lead to feelings of sadness and hopelessness.\u00a0\n\nFor many, it\u2019s hard to tell where ADHD ends and depression begins.\n\n### Does ADHD Cause Depression?\n\nWhile ADHD doesn\u2019t directly \u201ccause\u201d depression, there\u2019s a strong connection between the two.\u00a0\n\nResearchers have found that adults with ADHD are more likely to experience depression than those without it.\u00a0\n\nHere\u2019s why:\n\nChronic Frustration: ADHD makes everyday tasks\u2014like managing time, remembering commitments, or meeting deadlines\u2014more difficult. Repeated failures can lead to feelings of inadequacy.\nEmotional Dysregulation: People with ADHD often struggle to manage their emotions. Small setbacks can feel overwhelming, and it\u2019s easy to spiral into negative thinking.\nSocial Struggles: ADHD can make relationships difficult, whether it\u2019s forgetting important dates, interrupting conversations, or struggling to listen attentively. Over time, these challenges can create feelings of loneliness and rejection.\n\n\u00a0\n\nThese factors combine to create a cycle where ADHD symptoms fuel depressive feelings, and depression makes ADHD harder to manage.\n\n### \u00a0\n\n### The Emotional Weight of ADHD\n\nOne of the most misunderstood aspects of ADHD is its impact on emotions. ADHD doesn\u2019t just make you distracted; it affects how you process and react to feelings. People with ADHD often experience:\n\nIntense Emotions: Small events can trigger big feelings, like anger, sadness, or frustration.\nDifficulty Recovering: Once upset, it can take much longer to calm down than it does for others.\nShame and Guilt: Feeling \u201cdifferent\u201d or \u201cnot good enough\u201d can lead to a cycle of self-criticism.\n\n\u00a0\n\nDepression in adults with ADHD doesn\u2019t always look like sadness. Sometimes, it shows up as:\n\nExhaustion: A constant feeling of being tired, even when you haven\u2019t done much.\nLoss of Interest: Hobbies or activities you used to enjoy might feel meaningless.\nDifficulty Concentrating: ADHD already affects focus, but depression can make it worse.\nNegative Self-Talk: Thoughts like, \u201cI\u2019ll never get it together,\u201d or \u201cWhy even bother?\u201d\n\n\u00a0\n\nA patient once shared: \u201cIt felt like I was failing at life. No matter how hard I tried, it was never enough. Eventually, I just stopped trying.\u201d\n\nThese emotional struggles aren\u2019t \u201call in your head.\u201d They\u2019re real and rooted in how ADHD affects your brain and your life.\n\n### \u00a0\n\n### Best Therapy for ADHD and Depression\n\nThe most effective approach often combines treatments for both ADHD and depression.\u00a0\n\nFor example:\n\n[Cognitive Behavioral Therapy](/cognitive-behavioral-therapy) (CBT): Helps address negative thought patterns and improve time management.\nDialectical Behavior Therapy (DBT): Focuses on emotional regulation and mindfulness, especially helpful for ADHD\u2019s intense emotions.\nBehavioral Activation: Encourages small, positive actions to combat depression\u2019s inertia.\n\n\u00a0\n\nIn some cases, medication can play an important role.\u00a0\n\nRemember: You are not your struggles. ADHD and depression don\u2019t define you\u2014they\u2019re just parts of your journey. With [therapy for depression](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/treatments/depression-therapy/), you can learn to manage it and reclaim your life.\n\nIn the next chapter, we\u2019ll explore how to break the stigma around mental health and take the brave step of asking for help.\n\n## \u00a0\n\n## Chapter 4: Breaking the Stigma and Getting Help\n\nAsking for help can feel like the hardest thing to do when you\u2019re already struggling. You might wonder, What will people think? or What if I\u2019m just making excuses?\u00a0\n\nBut here\u2019s the truth: seeking help isn\u2019t a weakness\u2014it\u2019s one of the bravest things you can do for yourself.\n\nLiving with untreated ADHD, anxiety, or depression is like trying to run a marathon with a backpack full of bricks. The bricks\u2014shame, frustration, fear\u2014won\u2019t disappear on their own. But with the right support, you can start taking them out one by one.\n\n### Why Is There So Much Stigma?\n\nMany people hesitate to get help because of the stigma around mental health. Society often teaches us that we should \u201cjust try harder\u201d or \u201cpush through\u201d when we\u2019re struggling. For adults with ADHD, this message is even louder.\n\nFor years, ADHD was seen as a \u201ckids\u2019 problem.\u201d Many adults were told they couldn\u2019t possibly have it because they \u201cweren\u2019t hyper\u201d or had managed to hold a job. The truth is, ADHD doesn\u2019t go away when you grow up\u2014it just looks different.\n\nStigma often feeds off myths, like:\n\n\u201cADHD isn\u2019t real; it\u2019s just laziness.\u201d\n\u201cIf you need therapy or medication, something\u2019s wrong with you.\u201d\n\u201cEveryone feels anxious or distracted sometimes; why make it a big deal?\u201d\n\n\u00a0\n\nThese messages can make it hard to take the first step toward help. But here\u2019s the good news: you don\u2019t have to face stigma alone. By learning more about ADHD, anxiety, and depression, you can challenge these myths and empower yourself.\n\n### The Power of Getting Help\n\nImagine for a moment that your brain is like a car. ADHD means that your car doesn\u2019t come with GPS\u2014it\u2019s harder to know where to go or how to get there. Anxiety might add constant alarms, warning you about every possible danger, even when the road is clear. Depression might make you feel like you\u2019ve run out of gas entirely.\n\nHelp is like getting the tools to fix your car. It won\u2019t make the road perfect, but it will give you the support you need to navigate it.\n\nHere\u2019s how getting help can make a difference:\n\nUnderstanding Yourself: A therapist or doctor can help you see how ADHD, anxiety, and depression interact. Knowing why you feel the way you do is a powerful step toward change.\nLearning Strategies: Professionals can teach you coping strategies tailored to your needs, like creating routines, managing emotions, or breaking tasks into smaller steps.\nMedication When Needed: If recommended, medication can help regulate your brain\u2019s chemistry, making it easier to focus and manage emotions.\n\n### \u00a0\n\n### You Deserve to Feel Better\n\nIf you\u2019re reading this and feeling hesitant, that\u2019s okay. Taking the first step is always the hardest. ./server/blog-posts-data.json:2108:    "content": "## 5 Proven Strategies on How to Focus Better with ADHD\nIf you've ever found it challenging to finish a difficult job at work, studied for an important test, or worked on a challenging assignment, you may have wished you could Improve your Concentration and Focus. For individuals with ADHD, these challenges can feel even more overwhelming, as maintaining attention on a single task is often more difficult.\n\nConcentration is the mental effort you put into whatever you're currently learning or working on. It is frequently confused with attention span, which concerns how long you can concentrate on anything. ADHD can cause shifts in both. [According to Cleaveland Clinic,](https://my.clevelandclinic.org/health/diseases/4784-attention-deficithyperactivity-disorder-adhd) individuals with ADHD often find it harder to block out distractions, and factors like age or sleep deprivation can further affect focus.\n\nAs people age, they tend to forget things more quickly, and memory loss might be accompanied by decreased attention. \n\nFocus can also be impacted by ADHD, head or brain traumas (such as concussions), and other mental health conditions. When you're trying to focus but are having trouble, it can be stressful and frustrating, making it hard to concentrate on crucial work.\n\nIf this sounds like you, keep reading to learn more about scientifically supported strategies to improve concentration and focus, especially for those with ADHD.\n\n### Meditation\nResearchers from Harvard University discovered that 47% of the time, we are lost in contemplation. These thoughts are usually the result of students dwelling on the past or worrying about the future. For individuals with ADHD, this tendency to get lost in thought can be even more pronounced. Whereas worrying about the future can lead to tension and anxiety, reflecting on the past can produce feelings of melancholy and depression. Emotions become too much to handle if they are neglected.\n\nYou can overcome the never-ending \"what could've been\" and \"what ifs\" with meditation.\n\nResearchers at Columbia University Medical Center claim that via relaxation, meditation can alter the structure and functionality of the brain.\n\nBenefits of meditation include:\n\nIt Lessens melancholy, tension, and worry.Boost concentration and learning focus.Boost your attention span and memory.Boost your immune system and your level of psychological and physical resistance.Permit improved slumber.### Exercise\nAny form of strenuous physical activity increases blood flow to the brain. It then activates your neurons, promoting the formation of new cells, particularly in the hippocampal area. For individuals with ADHD, even 20 minutes of physical activity before studying or working can significantly improve focus and concentration.\n\nExercise raises endorphin levels. This chemical reduces stress levels, which improves brain focus and concentration. ADHD makes people more susceptible to stress, and frequent exercise can help in managing that stress and maintaining a positive outlook.Frequent exercise will assist you in managing stress and maintaining a good outlook if you're feeling overwhelmed.Researchers found a connection between excellent academic focus and physical wellness. Prolonged periods of concentration benefit greatly from low-intensity exercise, which raises our energy levels.\u00a0Studies have also shown that exercise boosts mental insight and creativity\u2014perfect for someone with ADHD who might be looking for a motivational breakthrough.Now that you know how exercise boosts brain power, why not get moving and go for a run, jog, or walk?\n\n### Sleep\nA sleepless night might make you cranky the next day. Sleep deprivation can affect your emotions in the morning and your brain over time. You spend about one-third of your waking hours sleeping, so it's a necessary part of your daily routine. Like food and water, getting enough sleep at the proper intervals is essential for survival.\u00a0\n\nSleep is required to establish and maintain brain circuits that support learning and memory formation. It also makes it harder to focus and react quickly. Getting enough sleep can help with many conditions, so do your brain a favor and get the necessary ZZZs.\n\nSleep is essential for memory and learning. Not getting enough sleep makes it hard to focus and learn new things. Your brain does not have enough time to store memories properly for subsequent retrieval.\u00a0Your brain can catch up as you sleep, preparing you for the next task.Lack of sleep saps your motivation and reduces your energy and time for muscle renewal. You'll experience slower reaction times and a more complex mental and physical effort. You perform at your best when you get enough sleep.Your brain is most likely to start processing memories while you are asleep. Your brain works to cement the memories you create during the day as you sleep.Even when your brain is entirely focused on falling asleep, it retains some degree of consciousness.## **Sleep and ADHD**\n[ADHD can significantly affect sleep](https://www.webmd.com/add-adhd/adult-adhd-and-sleep-problems). Many individuals with ADHD experience sleep disturbances such as difficulty falling asleep, staying asleep, or waking up too early.\u00a0\n\nSome common ways ADHD affects sleep include:\n\n**1. Difficulty Falling Asleep (Sleep-Onset Insomnia)**\n\nPeople with ADHD often have trouble winding down at night. Their minds may continue racing with thoughts, making it difficult to relax and fall asleep. Hyperactivity and impulsivity may also contribute to restlessness, preventing a smooth transition into sleep.\n\n**2. Restless Sleep**\n\nIndividuals with ADHD might experience restless sleep, characterized by frequent waking, tossing and turning, or difficulty maintaining a deep, restful sleep. This can leave them feeling tired and unrefreshed in the morning, even after a full night in bed.\n\n**3. Delayed Sleep-Wake Phase Disorder**\n\nMany people with ADHD have delayed sleep-wake phase disorder, meaning their natural circadian rhythm is shifted later, causing them to feel more awake at night and struggle with early morning wake-ups. This can result in inconsistent sleep patterns.\n\n**4. Increased Prevalence of Sleep Disorders**\n\nADHD is often linked with sleep disorders such as:\n\nSleep apnea: Obstructive sleep apnea, where breathing is briefly and repeatedly interrupted during sleep, can be more common in people with ADHD.Restless legs syndrome (RLS): Some individuals with ADHD report having an urge to move their legs at night, which can interfere with sleep.Periodic limb movement disorder (PLMD): This condition causes involuntary movements of the limbs during sleep, leading to disrupted rest.5. Medication Side Effects\n\nStimulant medications, commonly prescribed for ADHD, can sometimes lead to sleep difficulties, particularly if taken later in the day. These medications can make it harder to fall asleep, despite improving focus during waking hours.\n\n6. Impact on Sleep Hygiene\n\nPeople with ADHD may struggle with maintaining good sleep hygiene practices, such as sticking to a consistent sleep schedule, limiting screen time before bed, or creating a relaxing bedtime routine, all of which are important for healthy sleep.\n\n### How Sleep Problems Affect ADHD Symptoms\nLack of sleep can exacerbate core ADHD symptoms such as inattention, impulsivity, and hyperactivity.Sleep deprivation worsens emotional regulation, memory, and cognitive function, making it harder to manage ADHD during the day.Improving sleep in individuals with ADHD often involves a combination of behavioral strategies, lifestyle adjustments, and, in some cases, medication under a healthcare provider's guidance. Prioritizing sleep can significantly improve ADHD symptoms and overall quality of life.\n\n### \u00a0\n### Distraction-free environment\nYour environment dramatically impacts your ability to concentrate. If there are many distractions around you, you'll need to focus more, so try to make your workspace as distraction-free as possible.\n\nThere are several methods to do this.\n\nShut down your messaging apps like Slack, Skype, and email. The relentless barrage of notifications from these applications will be annoying if you leave them open.To prevent becoming sidetracked by incoming calls or texts, mute your phone.\u00a0Close the door to your office if you have one. It will shut out outside noise and let your colleagues know you are focused.If you don't have an office, avoid the noise by donning headphones and listening to music or other sounds.Turn off the TV if you are working from home.Clear your desk so that various people can handle it and not sidetrack you.## **Avoid multitasking**\nBecause multitasking requires mental energy to transition between tasks, it hinders concentration. Your brain finds it difficult to transition between tasks. A time interval known as the \"switching cost\" occurs each time you switch tasks since your brain has to pause working on one task and begin working on another. As a result, both tasks require much more time and are of lower quality.\n\nThe International Journal of Information Management reported on a study that found most people check their email every five minutes and that it takes them 64 seconds on average to get back to work after checking email. A great deal of time was wasted! Give your full, undivided attention to whatever you're doing.\u00a0\n\nWhen you multitask, your brain cannot entirely focus on any job because it continuously switches back and forth.Single-tasking allows you to focus on the task at hand, relieving you of the cognitive strain and distractions of multitasking.\u00a0When there is less constant switching between tasks, more energy may be put toward worthwhile endeavors. It's okay to focus again after every disruption.To improve your focus, stop multitasking and start single-tasking.\u00a0\n\nAchieving better focus improves your quality of life in general. You can accomplish your most significant objectives more quickly by focusing on your most crucial duties. You're less scattered and anxious and can go on steadily with the things that matter most to you.\n\nYou feel better about yourself, have more robust connections, and are more productive.\n\nIf you are having trouble focusing, don't give up. Between long hours, back-to-back patients, and life outside of work, the idea of growing your career might seem far away. But if you\u2019ve ever thought about doing more, whether that means moving into leadership, education, or public health, you\u2019re not alone.\r\n\r\nThe truth is, many nurses want to grow but don\u2019t know where to start. The good news? You don\u2019t have to overhaul your whole life to move forward. It\u2019s possible to build a future you\u2019re excited about without sacrificing everything else in the process.\r\n\r\nLet\u2019s look at how to grow in your nursing career while still having time for the rest of your life.\r\n## **1. Learning While Working: What Makes It Possible?**\n\r\nYou don\u2019t have to quit your job or take a break from life to go back to school. That might have been the case years ago, but now there are more flexible options made just for working professionals.\r\n\r\nOnline programs have opened the door for nurses who want to keep working while building new skills. These programs understand the demands of healthcare work. They\u2019re made with the idea that your schedule isn\u2019t 9 to 5. You might work nights. You might be on-call. Some weeks are easier than others. Many of these programs are 100% online and allow you to move through the material at your own pace.\r\n\r\nThis kind of learning fits into your life, not the other way around. It\u2019s all about choosing the right program, one that respects your time, your role, and your goals.\r\n## **2. Setting Career Goals That Fit Real Life**\n\r\nIt\u2019s easy to get caught up in what others are doing. But your path should work for you. Growth doesn\u2019t have to mean becoming a manager right away or moving into a whole new department. Sometimes it means learning how to do what you already do, better, safer, and with more confidence.\r\n\r\nStart by asking yourself: What kind of work do I enjoy? Maybe your short-term goal is to take a new certification or shadow someone in a role you\u2019re curious about. One major study found that 67.98% of people improved with therapy, 33.83% showed significant improvement, and 34.15% showed some progress. Being in a safe, consistent physical space with your therapist helps your brain learn that you're truly safe. In person therapy allows therapists to use body-based techniques that help process trauma stored in your body.\r\n\r\nSevere Depression: When depression is severe, it can be hard to feel connected to anything or anyone. Having your therapist physically present during anxiety episodes can provide immediate grounding and safety. They can guide you through breathing exercises and calming techniques more effectively.\r\n\r\nAutism Spectrum Disorders: Many people with autism benefit from the structure and routine of in person appointments. The consistent environment helps reduce anxiety, and therapists can better observe and work with sensory needs.\r\n\r\nPersonality Disorders: Building trust and healthy relationships is often a key part of treating personality disorders. The deeper connection possible through in person therapy can be crucial for healing relationship patterns.\r\n\r\nSubstance Use Disorders: Accountability is important in addiction recovery. In person sessions make it harder to avoid appointments and easier for therapists to notice signs of relapse.\r\n## \n\r\n### **What Therapists Say About In Person Sessions**\n\r\nMental health professionals have strong opinions about the value of face-to-face therapy. Dr. Robert Glenn, a licensed clinical psychologist in Orlando, explains: \"There's an energy exchange that happens when you're physically present with someone that you just can't replicate through a screen. I can sense my client's nervous system state much more accurately when they're sitting across from me.\"\r\n\r\nLicensed therapist Karla Mcleod notes: \"In my 8 years of practice, I've seen how the ritual of coming to therapy like the drive, the waiting room, the transition into my office, actually helps people shift into therapeutic mode. It's harder to achieve that mental shift when you're logging in from your kitchen table.\"\r\n### The Home Environment Challenge\n\r\nYour home is full of distractions, even when you don't realize it. On these days, it's important to be compassionate with yourself and prioritize your well-being. Pushing through severe depression without acknowledging how you feel can make things worse in the long run.\r\n\r\nHere are some strategies that might help when you're trying to work while feeling severely depressed:\r\n### Communicate (If You Feel Safe and Able)\n\r\nIf you have a supportive employer and feel safe doing so, consider letting them know you're having a difficult time. You don't need to share every detail, but a simple \"I'm having a challenging day with my health and may need some flexibility\" can open the door for understanding and potential adjustments.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIf you have an HR department, they might be a good resource to talk about options like taking a leave of absence if needed.\r\n\r\nThis is also a good time to reflect on [how to stop people pleasing](https://empathyhealthclinic.com/how-to-stop-people-pleasing/), especially if you find yourself constantly saying \u201cyes\u201d to avoid disappointing others, even when you're overwhelmed. Prioritising your mental health sometimes means setting boundaries and asking for support without guilt.\r\n\r\nLearning to advocate for your needs is a powerful step toward both healing and maintaining your well-being at work.\r\n### Focus on Small, Essential Tasks\n\r\nWhen everything feels overwhelming, break your work down into the absolute smallest, most necessary tasks. What is the one thing that absolutely has to get done today?\r\n\r\nFocus only on that. **Completing even one small task** can feel like a huge accomplishment when you're severely depressed.\r\n### Lower Your Expectations\n\r\nThis is not the time to aim for peak productivity or creativity. **Lower your expectations for yourself**. It's okay if you're not working at your usual pace or producing your best work. The goal is simply to get through the essential parts of the workday as gently as possible.\r\n### Take Frequent Breaks\n\r\nDon't try to power through for hours on end. Take short, frequent breaks. **Step away from your workspace**, do some stretching, listen to calming music, or just close your eyes for a few minutes. These breaks can help prevent burnout and make the work periods more manageable.\r\n### Create a Supportive Work Environment (Even at Home)\n\r\nSince you're working remotely, try to make your workspace as comfortable and supportive as possible. This might mean adjusting the lighting, having comforting items nearby, or playing soft, instrumental music. **Minimize distractions** as much as possible.\r\n### Consider Taking Time Off\n\r\nIf your severe depression is making it nearly impossible to function, it might be necessary to take a sick day or a short-term leave of absence. Your mental health is just as important as your physical health, and sometimes you need time to focus on getting better without the added pressure of work.\r\n\r\nLook into your **company's policies** regarding sick leave or disability.\r\n### Revisit Your Treatment Plan\n\r\n**Severe depression** is a sign that you may need to check in with your mental health professional. It might be time to adjust your treatment plan, try a different approach, or increase the frequency of your therapy sessions.\r\n\r\nWorking when severely depressed is incredibly challenging. **Be kind to yourself,** prioritize your well-being, and utilize your support systems.\r\n## **Signs a Job Isn\u2019t Good for Your Mental Health**\n\r\nSome jobs can make depression worse.
